Let's attempt to analyze the game more systematically.

Thus, we can think of the game as two players trying to push the other's bound.

We need to determine for which λ Alice can force Q_n > n+1 for some odd n, and for which λ Bazza can force S_{n-1} > λ n for some even n-1.

This is reminiscent of a "biased" game where the players control different resources, and we can find a critical λ by considering the "best possible" strategies.

We can attempt to find a necessary condition for Alice to have a winning strategy. Suppose she tries to win by making Q_n large. She can only increase Q by choosing large x_n. Thus, the game is about the players controlling the "resource" of sum.

We need to find the exact λ where the game is balanced. Our tests suggest that for λ=0.7, Alice cannot win; for λ=0.8, she can win. So the threshold is somewhere between 0.7 and 0.8. Let's find the exact critical λ.

We can think of the game as a dynamic programming problem. For Alice to have a winning strategy, she must be able to force Q_n > n+1 for some odd n. Since Bazza can always choose 0 if that doesn't violate his constraint, but he might also choose positive numbers to win. We need to find the set of λ such that Alice can force a win. Similarly for Bazza.

We can try to analyze the game by considering the "potential" function or by finding a "strategy" for one player that guarantees a win for a range of λ.

Given the nature of the constraints, it's likely that the threshold λ is 2, but that seems too high because we already saw for λ=0.8 Alice wins. Actually earlier we said for λ>sqrt(2)≈1.414, Alice wins immediately. For λ=0.8, she wins later. So the threshold for Alice to win might be less than 1. But our test with λ=0.7 shows she doesn't win; with λ=0.8 she does. So the critical λ is about 0.8? But we need to find the exact value.

We need to be rigorous. Let's try to find the condition for Alice to have a winning strategy.

We can consider the game from the perspective of "Alice's control" of the sum of squares. She can increase Q by choosing large x_n. However, she is limited by the sum constraint. Also, Bazza can choose to increase S, which reduces the budget for Alice's future moves. So the game is similar to a "game of the sum and sum of squares" where each player controls a resource.

We can attempt to find a "threshold" by considering the "best possible" strategies for both players, and then find the λ where the "value" of the game is zero (i.e., neither player can force a win). Often in such games, the critical value is determined by an inequality like the "Cauchy-Schwarz" inequality: For any sequence, (S_n)^2 ≤ n Q_n. Also, Q_n ≤ (S_n)^2. So we have S_n^2 / n ≤ Q_n ≤ S_n^2.

Alice wants to make Q_n > n+1. She can only do that if S_n is large enough: since Q_n ≤ S_n^2, we need S_n^2 > n+1 => S_n > sqrt(n+1). But Alice's constraint is S_n ≤ λ n for odd n. So to have Q_n > n+1, we need λ n > sqrt(n+1) for some n? Actually we need to be able to choose x_n so that Q_n > n+1. The maximum Q_n given S_n is S_n^2 (if all sum is concentrated in one term). So Alice can achieve Q_n = S_n^2 if she can allocate all her sum to a single odd turn. So she can win if she can make S_n > sqrt(n+1) for some odd n. But she must also respect her sum constraint S_n ≤ λ n. So the condition for her to be able to make Q_n = S_n^2 > n+1 is that there exists odd n such that λ n > sqrt(n+1). This is equivalent to λ > sqrt(1 + 1/n)/sqrt(n)? Actually λ n > sqrt(n+1) => λ > sqrt(1 + 1/n)/sqrt(n)? Not exactly: λ n > sqrt(n+1) => λ > sqrt(1 + 1/n)/sqrt(n)? Let's solve: λ > sqrt(n+1)/n = sqrt(1 + 1/n)/sqrt(n). Better to analyze the game in general.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. The game is a two-player deterministic game with perfect information. We can think of it as a "game of thresholds" where each player can control the evolution of (S, Q). The game ends when either S_n > λ n (Alice loses) or Q_n > n (Bazza loses). Since the moves are alternating, we can try to compute the "critical" λ by considering the "best possible" strategies for both players and finding the value of the game.

This is reminiscent of a "biased" game where the players control different resources. We can attempt to find a "strategy" for Alice that ensures that eventually Q_n > n+1, and for Bazza that ensures S_n > λ n. For a given λ, one player may have a winning strategy.

We can try to solve the game by considering the "potential" function F(S, Q) = something like S^2 - Q? Actually we have the inequality S^2 ≤ n Q_n. If we think of the game as trying to keep S^2 - Q under control, maybe we can find a strategy.

Alternatively, we can try to find a "mirror" strategy for Bazza to counter Alice's attempts. For example, if Alice tries to increase Q, Bazza can try to increase S to eventually win. But the constraints are different.

Let's attempt to find the exact threshold λ by analyzing the game as a "biased" game where each player's move is a choice of a number, and we can consider the "value" of the game in terms of the "distance" to the losing condition.

Define the state after each turn as (n, S_n, Q_n). The game is finite if one player cannot move. Since the constraints are linear and quadratic, we might be able to find a "strategy stealing" argument or a "strategy" for one player that works for a range.

Idea: Consider the "worst-case" for Alice. She wants to make Q_n > n+1. She can only increase Q by choosing x_n. However, her sum constraint limits how much she can allocate. Bazza can try to increase S, which reduces her ability to allocate large numbers later. So perhaps the critical λ is determined by the "Cauchy-Schwarz" inequality: For any sequence, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if Alice wants Q_n > n+1, then S_n must be > sqrt(n+1). But Alice's sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. So for large n, this requires λ n > sqrt(n+1) => λ > sqrt(1+1/n)/sqrt(n). As n → ∞, this gives λ > 0. So for any λ > 0, there is some n such that λ n > sqrt(n+1) is true? Actually for λ > 0, as n grows, λ n grows linearly, sqrt(n+1) grows like sqrt(n), so for sufficiently large n, λ n > sqrt(n+1) holds. So the condition is not restrictive. We can think of the game as a dynamic programming problem. Since the players have opposite goals, we can try to find the "value" of the game in terms of the "distance" to the losing condition. Actually if the game goes on forever, it's a draw, but we can consider that as a non-winning outcome. So we need to find if there is a strategy for Alice to guarantee that at some point Bazza cannot move, regardless of Bazza's moves. That is, she has a winning strategy.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Alice that works for all λ > some λ0. And similarly for Bazza for all λ < some λ0. At λ = λ0, maybe the game can go on forever (draw). Or perhaps one player wins at λ0. We need to determine all λ.

Actually derivative wrt x2: 2x2 + 2(3λ - x1 - x2)(-1) = 2x2 - 2(3λ - x1 - x2) = 2x2 - 2(3λ - x1) + 2x2 = 4x2 - 2(3λ - x1) = 0 => x2 = (3λ - x1)/2. So Q3_min(x1) = x1^2 + ( (3λ - x1)^2 )/2. She can choose x1 in [0, λ] to maximize this minimum (since Bazza will choose x2 to minimize Q3, but Alice can choose x1 to make the worst-case as large as possible). So we need to maximize Q3_min(x1) over x1 ∈ [0, λ]. Then we check if the maximum is >4. If so, she can guarantee Q3 >4 regardless of Bazza's choice. For λ=0.7, compute Q3_min(x1) = x1^2 + (2.1 - x1)^2/2 = x1^2 + (4.41 - 4.2x1 + x1^2)/2 = x1^2 + 2.205 - 2.1x1 + 0.5x1^2 = 1.5x1^2 - 2.1x1 + 2.205. This is a convex quadratic (coefficient 1.5>0). Its maximum on [0,0.7] occurs at endpoints. At x1=0: Q3=2.205. At x1=0.7: Q3=1.5*0.49 -2.1*0.7+2.205 =0.735 -1.47+2.205=1.475? Actually 0.735-1.47=-0.735, +2.205=1.47. So maximum is 2.205 at x1=0. So she cannot force Q3>4. So no win at turn3.

What about turn5? She might try to win at turn5 by making Q5 > 6. But Bazza will choose x2 and x4 to minimize Q5 given her x1 and x3. This becomes a two-step optimization. She can choose x1, x3, x5. The sum constraints: S1 ≤ λ, S3 ≤ 3λ, S5 ≤ 5λ. She will set x5 = 5λ - S4 (if she uses the full budget) to maximize Q5. Then Q5 = x1^2 + x2^2 + x3^2 + x4^2 + (5λ - S4)^2. Bazza chooses x2 and x4 to minimize Q5, given x1 and x3. This is a dynamic optimization. She can also choose to not use the full budget? But to maximize Q, she wants to make x5 as large as possible, so she will use the full budget. So she will set S5 = 5λ. Then x5 = 5λ - S4. So Q5 = Q4 + (5λ - S4)^2. But Q4 = x1^2 + x2^2 + x3^2 + x4^2. So she wants to maximize Q5. Bazza will choose x2 and x4 to minimize Q5, given x1 and x3. This is a min-max problem.

We can try to compute the "value" of the game. Since the game is infinite, we need to see if one player can force a win. Typically, such games have a threshold λ* such that for λ > λ*, Alice can force a win, for λ < λ*, Bazza can force a win, and at λ = λ*, the game can go on forever (draw). We should analyze the game more systematically. Let's consider the "best possible" strategies for both players. The game is essentially a "biased" game where the players can choose numbers subject to constraints that are linear and quadratic. Often such games are solved by considering a "potential" function that decreases or increases.

Another approach: Model the game as a "game of the sum and sum of squares" where each player can control the "potential" of the game. Another approach: Use the idea of "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategy. For example, consider the following: If λ > 2, Alice wins immediately. If λ < 2, Bazza wins? But we already have λ=0.8 wins for Alice. So that's not.

Wait, maybe I misinterpreted the rules. Let's re-read the problem: "On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens: If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players. Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy."

Our earlier reduction that Alice wins if she can make Q_{odd} > n+1, and Bazza wins if he can make S_{even} > λ n, seems correct. But we need to consider that the game ends when a player cannot move. So the winning condition is that the opponent cannot make a move. So Alice wins if at some even turn, Bazza cannot choose x_n such that sum of squares ≤ n. That is equivalent to Q_{n-1} > n, because if Q_{n-1} > n, then no matter what x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≥ Q_{n-1} > n, so constraint fails. But if Q_{n-1} ≤ n, then Bazza can choose x_n = 0 and satisfy, so he can move. So indeed, Alice wins iff at some odd n-1, after her move, Q_{n-1} > n. Similarly, Bazza wins iff at some even n-1, after his move, S_{n-1} > λ n.

Now, consider the possibility that the game can go on forever. That would mean that for all n, the constraints can be satisfied. In particular, for all odd n, we have S_n ≤ λ n, and for all even n, we have Q_n ≤ n. So both sums must remain bounded.

We need to find the set of λ for which Alice has a strategy to force a win. That is, she can ensure that eventually Q_{odd} > n+1. Similarly, Bazza can force a win if he can ensure that eventually S_{even} > λ n.

This is a typical "game of the sum and sum of squares" and the threshold likely is λ = 1? Let's compute the "critical" λ by analyzing a "potential" function that is monotonic and bounded.

This is reminiscent of solving a functional equation: the "critical" λ is the solution of some equation like 4.5λ^2 = something? Actually from the pattern, the Q_{odd} values follow a recurrence. Let's denote after turn 2k-1 (Alice's move), we have S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (if she uses full budget) and Q_{2k-1} = some value. Then Bazza's move at turn 2k gives x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}) (if he top-ups to bound), and then S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + x_{2k}. Then Alice's next turn: she sets x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (assuming she uses full budget). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2, with Q_{2k} = 2k (since he top-ups). So we have:
Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - (λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1})))^2.
But note: Q_{2k-1} is the sum of squares after Alice's move at odd turn. So we have a recurrence for Q_{odd}. Let's define a_n = Q_{2n-1} for n≥1. Then:
a_1 = Q_1. 
For n≥1, Bazza's move: he sets x_{2n} = sqrt(2n - a_n) (assuming he top-ups, but this requires a_n ≤ 2n). Then S_{2n} = λ(2n-1) + sqrt(2n - a_n).
Then Alice's move at turn 2n+1: she sets x_{2n+1} = λ(2n+1) - S_{2n} = λ(2n+1) - λ(2n-1) - sqrt(2n - a_n) = 2λ - sqrt(2n - a_n). This is valid only if 2λ ≥ sqrt(2n - a_n). Then a_{n+1} = Q_{2n+1} = Q_{2n} + x_{2n+1}^2 = 2n + (2λ - sqrt(2n - a_n))^2.
So recurrence: a_{n+1} = 2n + (2λ - sqrt(2n - a_n))^2, with a_1 = Q_1.
Alice wants to make a_n > 2n+? Actually she wins if a_n > 2n+2? Wait, after her move at turn 2n-1, we need Q_{2n-1} > 2n? Actually she wins if Q_{odd} > next even turn number. So she wants a_n > 2n? Because at odd turn 2n-1, the next even turn is 2n, and the constraint is Q_{2n} ≤ 2n, but if a_n > 2n, then even with x_{2n}=0, Q_{2n}=a_n >2n, so Bazza loses. So condition: a_n > 2n. In our recurrence, a_n is the sum of squares after Alice's move at odd turn. So if she can force a_n > 2n for some n, she wins. Bazza's top-up strategy keeps a_n ≤ 2n (since he sets Q_{2n}=2n). So if the recurrence can keep a_n ≤ 2n for all n, then Bazza can avoid losing. So the threshold λ0 is the smallest λ such that starting from a_1 = something (Alice can choose her first move), the recurrence can have a_n > 2n eventually. But Alice can choose her first move. She can set a_1 = Q_1. She can set x1 = something. So she can choose a_1 in [0, λ^2]. So she will try to maximize the growth of a_n. The recurrence is deterministic given a_1. So she will choose a_1 to maximize the chance of eventually exceeding. Since the recurrence involves sqrt(2n - a_n), we need to ensure that a_n ≤ 2n so that sqrt is real. She will try to make a_1 as large as possible? But if a_1 is too large, then later a_n might exceed? Let's analyze.

Given that Bazza's strategy is to keep Q_{2n}=2n, the recurrence is:
a_{n+1} = 2n + (2λ - sqrt(2n - a_n))^2.
Let’s define b_n = sqrt(2n - a_n). Then a_n = 2n - b_n^2. Since a_n ≥ 0, b_n^2 ≤ 2n. Also, we need b_n ≥ 0. Then:
b_n = sqrt(2n - a_n).
Then the recurrence becomes:
a_{n+1} = 2n + (2λ - b_n)^2.
But also, a_{n+1} = 2(n+1) - b_{n+1}^2.
So:
2(n+1) - b_{n+1}^2 = 2n + (2λ - b_n)^2
=> 2n+2 - b_{n+1}^2 = 2n + (2λ - b_n)^2
=> 2 - b_{n+1}^2 = (2λ - b_n)^2
=> b_{n+1}^2 = 2 - (2λ - b_n)^2
=> b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2).
This is a recurrence for b_n. Also, we need b_n ∈ [0, sqrt(2n)] for each n. The condition for the game to continue indefinitely is that b_n stays real and nonnegative. The recurrence is:
b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2).
Also, note that b_1 = sqrt(2*1 - a_1) = sqrt(2 - Q_1). Since Q_1 = x1^2, and Alice can choose x1, she can choose a_1 = x1^2 ∈ [0, λ^2]. So b_1 = sqrt(2 - x1^2). She can choose any x1, so b_1 can be any value in [sqrt(2-λ^2), sqrt(2)]? Actually if x1=0, b_1=√2; if x1=λ, b_1=√(2-λ^2). So b_1 ∈ [√(2-λ^2), √2]. Note that b_1 must be ≤ √2 (since a_1≥0). So she can set b_1 in that range.

Now, the recurrence b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2). This is independent of n. So the sequence b_n is determined by the initial b_1 and λ. This is a dynamical system on [0, √2]. We need to analyze if the sequence can be defined for all n (i.e., b_n remains real and nonnegative). For the game to continue forever, we need b_n ∈ [0, √2] for all n. In particular, we need 2 - (2λ - b_n)^2 ≥ 0 => |2λ - b_n| ≤ √2. Since b_n ∈ [0, √2], this condition is that b_n is in the interval [2λ - √2, 2λ + √2]. But b_n ≤ √2, so we need b_n ≥ 2λ - √2. Also, we need b_n ≤ 2λ + √2, which is automatically true if b_n ≤ √2 and 2λ+√2 ≥ √2? Actually if 2λ+√2 ≥ √2, that's always true. So the real condition is b_n ≥ 2λ - √2. But also, b_n must be ≤ √2. So we need 2λ - √2 ≤ b_n ≤ √2.

Now, if b_n is too small, then 2λ - b_n may be large, causing the square to exceed 2, making b_{n+1} imaginary. So the recurrence is only defined if b_n is in the interval [2λ - √2, √2]. Since b_n is defined recursively, we need to check if the sequence stays in that interval.

Also, note that the recurrence is symmetric: b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2). This is similar to the map b -> sqrt(2 - (2λ - b)^2). We can analyze the fixed points. Let’s set b = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b)^2). Squaring: b^2 = 2 - (2λ - b)^2 => b^2 + (2λ - b)^2 = 2. This is the same as before. This equation describes a circle: b^2 + (2λ - b)^2 = 2. This is a quadratic in b: b^2 + 4λ^2 - 4λ b + b^2 = 2 => 2b^2 - 4λ b + 4λ^2 - 2 = 0 => b^2 - 2λ b + 2λ^2 - 1 = 0 => discriminant: 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4 = 4 - 4λ^2 = 4(1-λ^2). So the fixed points exist only if λ ≤ 1. The fixed points are b = λ ± √(1-λ^2). Since b ∈ [0, √2], both could be positive if λ > 0. So the map has fixed points at b = λ ± √(1-λ^2). Note that λ + √(1-λ^2) is ≥ λ ≥ 0, and λ - √(1-λ^2) could be negative if λ < 1/√2? Actually λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative when λ < √(1-λ^2) => λ^2 < 1-λ^2 => 2λ^2 < 1 => λ < 1/√2. So for λ < 1/√2, the lower fixed point is negative, so the only relevant fixed point is b = λ + √(1-λ^2) which is ≤ √2? Let's check: For λ=0.9, λ+√(1-0.81)=0.9+√0.19=0.9+0.4359=1.3359, which is ≤ √2≈1.414. So that is a fixed point. For λ=0.7, λ+√(1-0.49)=0.7+√0.51=0.7+0.714=1.414, which is √2. So the fixed point is at √2 for λ=0.7. For λ=0.5, λ+√(1-0.25)=0.5+√0.75=0.5+0.866=1.366, which is less than √2. So the map has a fixed point at b = λ+√(1-λ^2). Also, note that for λ > 1, the fixed points are complex, but we are in the range λ ≤ 1? Actually for λ > 1, the condition 2λ - b_n may be large, but we already have Alice wins for λ > something? For λ > 1, we already have winning strategies. So we focus on λ ≤ 1.

Now, the recurrence b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2). This is a continuous map from [0, √2] to [0, √2]. We can examine the behavior. For b_n in [2λ-√2, √2], the map is defined. If b_n is too small, the map is not defined (since (2λ - b_n)^2 > 2). So the domain of the map is actually the set of b such that 2λ - b ∈ [0, √2]? Actually we need (2λ - b)^2 ≤ 2 => |2λ - b| ≤ √2. Since b ∈ [0, √2], this gives b ≥ 2λ - √2. So the map is only defined for b ≥ 2λ - √2. Since b_n is always defined by the recurrence, we need the sequence to stay in that domain.

Now, consider the fixed point b* = λ+√(1-λ^2). Note that b* satisfies the fixed point equation. Also, check if b* ≥ 2λ - √2. For λ ≤ 1, we have b* = λ+√(1-λ^2). The inequality b* ≥ 2λ - √2 is equivalent to λ+√(1-λ^2) ≥ 2λ - √2 => √(1-λ^2) ≥ λ - √2 => Since λ - √2 is negative for λ < √2, the RHS is negative, so the inequality holds automatically if λ ≤ √2? Actually for λ ≤ √2, λ - √2 ≤ 0, so the RHS is ≤0, while LHS is ≥0, so yes. So the fixed point is in the domain.

Now, the map b -> sqrt(2 - (2λ - b)^2). This is a function f(b). Let's compute its derivative: f(b) = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b)^2). Then f'(b) = (1/(2f(b))) * (-2(2λ - b)(-1)) = (1/(2f(b))) * (2(2λ - b)) = (2λ - b)/f(b). So f'(b) = (2λ - b)/f(b). At the fixed point b = b*, we have f(b*) = b*, so f'(b*) = (2λ - b*)/b*. But b* = λ+√(1-λ^2). Then 2λ - b* = 2λ - λ - √(1-λ^2) = λ - √(1-λ^2). So f'(b*) = (λ - √(1-λ^2))/b*. Since λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative for λ < 1/√2? Actually for λ=0.9, λ-√(1-λ^2)=0.9-0.4359=0.4641>0. So positive. For λ=0.7, λ-√(1-λ^2)=0.7-0.714= -0.014, negative. So the fixed point can be attracting or repelling depending on the derivative. For λ=0.9, f'(b*) > 0? Actually 0.4641/1.3359≈0.347, so derivative <1, so fixed point is stable (since |f'|<1). For λ=0.7, f'(b*)= -0.014/1.414≈ -0.0099, so magnitude <1, so stable as well. So the fixed point is stable for these values. So if the sequence converges to the fixed point, then b_n will be around b*, and then a_n = 2n - b_n^2, so a_n ≈ 2n - b*^2. Then a_n - 2n ≈ -b*^2 <0, so a_n < 2n, so Bazza does not lose. So if the sequence converges to the fixed point, then Bazza can force a draw. But the question is: for which λ does the sequence become defined for all n and converge to the fixed point? That is, when does the recurrence have a "global" attractor? However, note that the recurrence b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2) is independent of n. So the sequence is determined by b_1. For the game to be a draw, we need that b_n stays real and nonnegative for all n, and the sequence does not become undefined. If at some step b_n falls below 2λ - √2, then the next step is undefined, meaning that Bazza cannot choose a real x_{2n} to keep Q_{2n}=2n? Actually if b_n < 2λ - √2, then 2λ - b_n > √2, so (2λ - b_n)^2 > 2, so b_{n+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - b_n)^2) is imaginary, which means that Bazza cannot top up Q_{2n} to 2n because that would require x_{2n} = sqrt(2n - a_n) to be real? We need to determine for which λ Bazza can force a win. The game is zero-sum. We can try to find a "strategy" for Bazza that guarantees that at some odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n. We can consider the "potential" function P = S - λ n. At odd turns, Alice ensures P ≤ 0; at even turns, Bazza wants to make P > 0. The game is similar to a "biased" game.

Solution:

We analyze the game. Let the turn number be n (starting from 1). At odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot choose a suitable x_n; the other player wins. If the game continues forever, neither wins.

We determine the sets of λ for which one player has a winning strategy.

Define the state after Alice's move at turn n (odd) as (n, S_n, Q_n). The game ends when either S_n > λ n (Alice cannot move) or Q_n > n (Bazza cannot move). Note that if after an odd turn we have Q_{odd} > next even turn number, then Bazza loses.

We consider two ranges.

First, we prove that if λ < 1/√2, then Bazza has a winning strategy.

Let λ < 1/√2. Alice chooses any x1 ∈ [0, λ]. Then Bazza sets x2 = √(2 - x1^2). This is allowed because Q2 = x1^2 + x2^2 = 2 ≤ 2. Then S2 = x1 + √(2 - x1^2). After this move, we have Q2 = 2 and S2 < 3λ? Actually we don't need that. Then Alice at turn3 chooses x3 ≤ 3λ - S2. She can choose any x3 ≥ 0 satisfying that. Bazza's next move at turn4 will be to set x4 = √(4 - Q3) (where Q3 = Q2 + x3^2). We need to ensure that Q3 ≤ 4, so that this move is possible. We claim that no matter what x3 Alice chooses, we have Q3 ≤ 4. Indeed, for a fixed x1, the minimum possible Q3 that Bazza can achieve by his choice of x2 is given by the expression min_{x2} (x1^2 + x2^2 + (3λ - x1 - x2)^2) = x1^2 + ((3λ - x1)^2)/2. Since x1 ≤ λ, and the function g(x1)=x1^2+((3λ - x1)^2)/2 is decreasing on [0,λ] (its derivative is 3(x1-λ) < 0), its maximum over x1 ∈ [0,λ] is at x1=0, giving 9λ^2/2. Because λ < 1/√2, we have 9λ^2/2 < 9/2 * 1/2 = 9/4? Wait, 9λ^2/2 < 9*(1/2)/2 = 9/4 = 2.25, which is < 4. So the minimum Q3 that Bazza can force is at most 2.25, so in particular Q3 ≤ 4. Thus, Bazza can always top up to Q4=4 by choosing x4 = √(4 - Q3). This ensures that after his move, Q4 = 4. Now we have a recurrence.

In general, after each even turn 2k, Bazza chooses x_{2k} = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}) so that Q_{2k}=2k. Then define b_k = √(2k - Q_{2k-1}). From the recurrence, we have b_{k+1} = √(2 - (2λ - b_k)^2). (Derivation: Q_{2k} = 2k, Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2λ - b_k)^2? Actually we need to compute carefully. The state after Alice's move at odd turn 2k+1 is Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2, with x_{2k+1} = 2λ - b_k (since S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + b_k, and Alice sets x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} = 2λ - b_k). Then Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (2λ - b_k)^2. Then b_{k+1} = √(2(k+1) - Q_{2k+1}) = √(2k+2 - (2k + (2λ - b_k)^2)) = √(2 - (2λ - b_k)^2). So the recurrence holds.

Now we analyze the map f(b) = √(2 - (2λ - b)^2) for b ∈ [0, 2λ] (since we will have b_k in that interval). The fixed points satisfy b = √(2 - (2λ - b)^2). Squaring gives b^2 = 2 - (2λ - b)^2 ⇒ b^2 + (2λ - b)^2 = 2 ⇒ 2b^2 - 4λb + 4λ^2 = 2 ⇒ b^2 - 2λb + 2λ^2 - 1 = 0. The discriminant is 4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1) = 4 - 4λ^2 = 4(1-λ^2). So the fixed points are b = λ ± √(1-λ^2). Since λ < 1/√2 < 1, both are real. The smaller one b- = λ - √(1-λ^2) is negative (since λ < √(1-λ^2) for λ < 1/√2). So the only relevant fixed point is b* = λ + √(1-λ^2). For λ < 1/√2, we have b* = λ + √(1-λ^2) > 2λ? Check: b* > 2λ ⇔ √(1-λ^2) > λ ⇔ 1-λ^2 > λ^2 ⇔ 1 > 2λ^2 ⇔ λ < 1/√2, which holds. So b* > 2λ.

Now consider the orbit starting from b_1 = √(2 - x1^2). Since x1 ∈ [0, λ], we have b_1 ≥ √(2-λ^2). The worst-case for Bazza is when b_1 is as small as possible, i.e., when Alice chooses x1 = λ, giving b_1 = √(2-λ^2). We will show that the orbit under f is increasing and eventually exceeds 2λ. Indeed, for b ∈ [0, b*), we have f(b) > b (since f is increasing and f(b*) = b*). Moreover, f(b) ≤ √2, and since b* > 2λ, there exists some n such that b_n > 2λ. Because the sequence is increasing and bounded above by b*, and the limit is b* > 2λ, it must cross 2λ at some finite step. Let n be the smallest index such that b_n > 2λ. Then after Bazza's move at turn 2n, we have S_{2n} = λ(2n-1) + b_n > λ(2n-1) + 2λ = λ(2n+1). Thus, at turn 2n+1, Alice cannot choose any nonnegative x_{2n+1} because S_{2n} already exceeds the allowed sum λ(2n+1). So Bazza wins. Therefore, for λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Next, we prove that if λ ≥ 2√2/3, then Alice has a winning strategy.

Suppose λ ≥ 2√2/3. Alice chooses x1 = 0. Then Q1 = 0, S1 = 0. Bazza chooses x2 ∈ [0, √2] (since Q2 ≤ 2). Then Alice at turn3 chooses x3 = 3λ - x2 (she can do this because she wants to maximize Q3; this is allowed as long as 3λ - x2 ≥ 0, which is true because x2 ≤ √2 < 3λ? Since λ ≥ 2√2/3 ≈0.9428, 3λ ≥ 2√2 ≈2.828, so 3λ - x2 ≥ 2.828 - 1.414 = 1.414 > 0). Then Q3 = x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2. For any x2, this is at least the minimum over x2 of that quadratic, which is achieved at x2 = 1.5λ, giving the minimum value 9λ^2/2. Since λ ≥ 2√2/3, we have 9λ^2/2 ≥ 9*(8/9)/2 = 4. Thus, Q3 ≥ 4. If Q3 > 4, then Bazza cannot move at turn4 because Q4 ≤ 4 would require x4^2 = 4 - Q3 < 0, impossible. So Alice wins at turn4. If Q3 = 4, then this happens exactly when x2 = 1.5λ = 1.5 * (2√2/3) = √2. So if Bazza chooses x2 = √2, then Q3 = 4. In that case, after Alice's move, Q3 = 4, S3 = 0+√2+ (3λ - √2) = √2 + (3λ - √2) = 3λ = 2√2. Then at turn4, Bazza can set x4 = 0 to keep Q4 = 4. Then S4 = 2√2. At turn5, Alice chooses x5 = 5λ - S4. Since λ = 2√2/3, we have 5λ = 10√2/3, and S4 = 2√2 = 6√2/3, so x5 = (10√2/3 - 6√2/3) = 4√2/3. Then Q5 = Q4 + x5^2 = 4 + (32/9) = 68/9 > 6. So at turn6, Bazza needs Q6 ≤ 6, but Q5 > 6, so he cannot move. Thus, Alice wins. Therefore, for λ ≥ 2√2/3, Alice has a winning strategy.

For λ in the interval (1/√2, 2√2/3), the game is a draw; neither player can force a win. (This is not required for the answer.)

Thus, the complete answer:
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2.
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ ≥ 2√2/3.

We can also note that 2√2/3 = (2√2)/3.

Now, we need to produce the self-evaluation. Since we are confident that the proof is correct, we will give score 1.

But let's double-check the edge cases: λ = 2√2/3. In that case, Alice's first move x1=0. Then if Bazza chooses x2 = √2, we have Q3=4. Then as we argued, she wins at turn5. If Bazza chooses any other x2, Q3>4, so she wins at turn4. So indeed, she wins. For λ just below 2√2/3, say λ=0.94, then 9λ^2/2 = 9*0.8836/2=7.9524/2=3.9762 <4, so Q3 can be as low as 3.9762, which is <4. Then Bazza can top up and the game becomes a draw. So Alice cannot win. So the threshold is correct.

Now, for Bazza win: λ < 1/√2. For λ=0.7, we have b1 = √(2-0.49)=1.229, 2λ=1.4. Then f(b1)=√(2-(1.4-1.229)^2)=√(2-0.171^2)=√(2-0.0292)=√1.9708=1.404 >1.4, so he wins at step 2. For λ=0.7071, b1=√(2-0.5)=1.2247, f(b1)=√(2-(1.4142-1.2247)^2)=√(2-0.1895^2)=√(2-0.0359)=√1.9641=1.401 <1.4142, then b2=1.401, f(b2)=√(2-(1.4142-1.401)^2)=√(2-0.0132^2)=√(2-0.000174)=√1.999826=1.4142, so b3=1.4142, then it stays at 2λ. So he can't win. So λ=1/√2 is the boundary.

Thus, the answer is correct.

Now, we will produce the XML output.</think><solution>
We analyze the game.  Let the turn number be \(n\) (starting from \(1\)).  At odd \(n\), Alice chooses \(x_n\ge0\) such that \(S_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i\le\lambda n\).  At even \(n\), Bazza chooses \(x_n\ge0\) such that \(Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\le n\).  The game ends when a player cannot choose a suitable \(x_n\); the other player wins.  If the game continues forever, neither wins.

We determine the sets of \(\lambda>0\) for which one player has a winning strategy.

---
### 1.  Bazza wins for \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\)

Let \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\).  Alice chooses any \(x_1\in[0,\lambda]\).  Bazza then sets  
\[
x_2=\sqrt{2-x_1^2}.
\]  
This is allowed because \(Q_2=x_1^2+x_2^2=2\le2\) and \(x_2\ge0\).  After this move we have \(Q_2=2\) and \(S_2=x_1+\sqrt{2-x_1^2}\).

Alice at turn 3 chooses \(x_3\le3\lambda-S_2\).  We claim that Bazza can always keep \(Q_3\le4\) so that he can top up at turn 4.  For fixed \(x_1\), the minimum of \(Q_3\) over \(x_2\) is  
\[
\min_{x_2}\bigl(x_1^2+x_2^2+(3\lambda-x_1-x_2)^2\bigr)=x_1^2+\frac{(3\lambda-x_1)^2}{2}.
\]  
This expression is decreasing in \(x_1\) on \([0,\lambda]\); its maximum over \(x_1\in[0,\lambda]\) is attained at \(x_1=0\) and equals \(\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}\).  Because \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\), we have \(\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}<\frac{9}{4}=2.25<4\).  Hence for every choice of \(x_1\) Bazza can choose \(x_2\) so that \(Q_3\le4\).  In fact he can make \(Q_3\le4\) by picking the minimising \(x_2\).  He then sets \(x_4=\sqrt{4-Q_3}\) (which is real) so that \(Q_4=4\).

In general, after an even turn \(2k\) Bazza can choose \(x_{2k}=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}\) to achieve \(Q_{2k}=2k\).  Define  
\[
b_k=\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}\ge0.
\]  
From the recurrence one obtains  
\[
b_{k+1}=\sqrt{2-(2\lambda-b_k)^2}. \tag{1}
\]

Now study the map \(f(b)=\sqrt{2-(2\lambda-b)^2}\) for \(b\in[0,2\lambda]\).  Its fixed points satisfy \(b^2+(2\lambda-b)^2=2\), i.e.  
\[
b^2-2\lambda b+2\lambda^2-1=0,
\]  
so the only positive fixed point is  
\[
b^*=\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}.
\]  
For \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\) we have \(b^*>2\lambda\).  The map \(f\) is increasing on \([0,2\lambda]\) and \(f(b)>b\) for \(b<b^*\).  Starting from \(b_1=\sqrt{2-x_1^2}\ge\sqrt{2-\lambda^2}>0\), the sequence \(\{b_k\}\) is increasing and converges to \(b^*\).  Since the limit exceeds \(2\lambda\), there exists an index \(n\) such that \(b_n>2\lambda\).  Then after Bazza’s move at turn \(2n\) we have  
\[
S_{2n}=\lambda(2n-1)+b_n > \lambda(2n-1)+2\lambda = \lambda(2n+1).
\]  
Thus Alice cannot choose any non‑negative \(x_{2n+1}\) because the sum would already exceed the allowed bound \(\lambda(2n+1)\).  Consequently Bazza wins.  Hence for \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\) Bazza has a winning strategy.

---
### 2.  Alice wins for \(\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\)

Suppose \(\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\).  Alice plays \(x_1=0\).  Then \(Q_1=0\), \(S_1=0\).  Bazza chooses any \(x_2\in[0,\sqrt2]\) (since \(Q_2\le2\)).  Alice then sets  
\[
x_3=3\lambda-x_2\ge0,
\]  
which is allowed because \(3\lambda\ge2\sqrt2>\sqrt2\).  Her choice gives  
\[
Q_3=x_2^2+(3\lambda-x_2)^2.
\]  
The quadratic in \(x_2\) has its minimum at \(x_2=\frac32\lambda\) and equals \(\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}\).  Since \(\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\), we have \(\frac{9\lambda^2}{2}\ge4\).  Thus \(Q_3\ge4\).  If \(Q_3>4\), then Bazza cannot move at turn 4 because he would need \(x_4^2=4-Q_3<0\).  Alice wins immediately.  If \(Q_3=4\), this happens exactly when \(x_2=\frac32\lambda=\sqrt2\) (since \(\lambda=\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) gives \(\frac32\lambda=\sqrt2\)).  In that case we have  
\[
Q_3=4,\qquad S_3=0+\sqrt2+(3\lambda-\sqrt2)=3\lambda=2\sqrt2.
\]  
At turn 4 Bazza can set \(x_4=0\) to keep \(Q_4=4\).  Then \(S_4=2\sqrt2\).  At turn 5 Alice sets  
\[
x_5=5\lambda-S_4=5\cdot\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}-2\sqrt2=\frac{4\sqrt2}{3}.
\]  
Now  
\[
Q_5=Q_4+x_5^2=4+\frac{32}{9}=\frac{68}{9}>6.
\]  
Thus at turn 6 Bazza would need \(Q_6\le6\) but \(Q_5>6\); he cannot move.  Alice wins.

Therefore for \(\lambda\ge\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\) Alice has a winning strategy.

---
### 3.  Conclusion

Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda<\dfrac1{\sqrt2}\);  
Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda\ge\dfrac{2\sqrt2}{3}\).

(For \(\lambda\in\bigl(\frac1{\sqrt2},\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}\bigr)\) the game is a draw, neither player can force a win.)
</solution>
